   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Djibouti</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Republic on the northeastern coast of Africa
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the country in the Horn of Africa. For its capital city, see <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">Djibouti (city)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Djibouti (disambiguation)">Djibouti (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div>

      <p><b>Djibouti</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[note 1]</a></sup> officially the <b>Republic of Djibouti</b>, is a country located in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> in the south, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> in the north, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> in the east. Across the Gulf of Aden is <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. The country has a total area of 23,200&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (8,958&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> The Republic of Djibouti is predominantly inhabited by two ethnic groups, the <a href="/wiki/Somalis" title="Somalis">Somali</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> people, with the former comprising the majority of the population. </p>
      <p>In antiquity, the territory together with Ethiopia, Eritrea and Somalia was part of the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a>. Nearby <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>, now in Somalia, was the seat of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifat Sultanate">Ifat</a> Sultanates. In the late 19th century, the colony of <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> was established following treaties signed by the ruling <a href="/wiki/Dir_(clan)" title="Dir (clan)">Dir</a> <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a> sultans with the French<sup id="cite_ref-Uwechue_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uwechue-6">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_Somaliland_Hist_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911_Somaliland_Hist-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-8">[7]</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Ethiopian_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Ethiopian Railway">railroad</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dire_Dawa" title="Dire Dawa">Dire Dawa</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>) allowed it to quickly supersede <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> as the port for southern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ogaden" title="Ogaden">Ogaden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-9">[8]</a></sup> It was subsequently renamed to the <a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a> in 1967. A decade later, the Djiboutian people voted for independence. This officially marked the establishment of the <i>Republic of Djibouti</i>, named after its <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">capital city</a>. The new state joined the United Nations the same year, on 20 September 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> In the early 1990s, tensions over government representation led to <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_Civil_War" title="Djiboutian Civil War">armed conflict</a>, which ended in a power-sharing agreement in 2000 between the ruling party and the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Djibouti is a multi-ethnic nation with a population of over 921,804 inhabitants (<a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Africa" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Africa">the smallest</a> in mainland Africa). Somali and Arabic are the country's two official languages. About 94% of residents adhere to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> which is the official religion and has been predominant in the region for more than a thousand years. The <a href="/wiki/Somalis" title="Somalis">Somalis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> make up the two largest ethnic groups. Both speak the <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Djibouti is strategically located near some of the world's busiest shipping lanes, controlling access to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>. It serves as a key refuelling and transshipment center, and is the principal maritime port for imports from and exports to neighboring <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. A burgeoning commercial hub, the nation is the site of various foreign military bases, including <a href="/wiki/Camp_Lemonnier" title="Camp Lemonnier">Camp Lemonnier</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Authority_on_Development" title="Intergovernmental Authority on Development">Intergovernmental Authority on Development</a> (IGAD) regional body also has its headquarters in Djibouti City.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_etymology">Name and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p><i>Djibouti</i> is officially known as the <i>Republic of Djibouti</i>. In local languages it is known as <i>Yibuuti</i> (in <a href="/wiki/Afar_language" title="Afar language">Afar</a>) and <i>Jabuuti</i> (in <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a>). </p>
      <p>The country is named for its capital, the <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">City of Djibouti</a>. The etymology of the name is disputed. Several theories and legends exist regarding its origin, varying based on ethnicity. One theory derives it from the <a href="/wiki/Afar_language" title="Afar language">Afar</a> word <i lang="aa-Latn" title="Afar-language romanization">gabouti</i>, meaning "plate", possibly referring to the geographical features of the area.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Another connects it to <i>gabood</i>, meaning "upland/plateau".<sup id="cite_ref-culturetrip_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-culturetrip-13">[12]</a></sup> Djibouti could also mean "Land of <i>Tehuti</i>" or "Land of <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a> (<a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a>: <i>Djehuti</i>/ <i>Djehuty</i>)", after the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Egyptian</a> Moon God.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From 1862 until 1894, the land to the north of the Gulf of Tadjoura was called "<a href="/wiki/Obock_Region" title="Obock Region">Obock</a>". Under French administration, from 1883 to 1967 the area was known as <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> (French: <i>Côte française des Somalis</i>), and from 1967 to 1977 as the <a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a> (French: <i>Territoire français des Afars et des Issas</i>). </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Djibouti" title="History of Djibouti">History of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/170px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/255px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG/340px-Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="766"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pottery_of_Asa_Koma.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Geometric design pottery found in <a href="/wiki/Asa_Koma" title="Asa Koma">Asa Koma</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> region has often been considered a primary crossing point for early hominins following a southern coastal route from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> to <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. </p>
      <p>The Djibouti area has been inhabited since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>. According to linguists, the first <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a>-speaking populations arrived in the region during this period from the family's proposed <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_Urheimat" title="Afroasiatic Urheimat">urheimat</a> ("original homeland") in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> Other scholars propose that the Afroasiatic family developed in situ in the Horn, with its speakers subsequently dispersing from there.<sup id="cite_ref-Blench143144_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blench143144-18">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Cut stones dated about 3 million years old have been collected in the area of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Abbe" title="Lake Abbe">Lake Abbe</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the Gobaad plain (between Dikhil and Lake Abbe), the remains of a <a href="/wiki/Palaeoloxodon_recki" title="Palaeoloxodon recki">Palaeoloxodon recki elephant</a> were also discovered, visibly butchered using basalt tools found nearby. These remains would date from 1.4 million years BCE. Subsequently, other similar sites were identified as probably the work of <a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> site (from 800,000 to 400,000 years BCE), where <a href="/wiki/Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone">stone</a> was cut, was excavated in the 1990s, in Gombourta, between <a href="/wiki/Damerdjog" class="mw-redirect" title="Damerdjog">Damerdjog</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loyada" title="Loyada">Loyada</a>, 15 km south of <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Djibouti City">Djibouti City</a>. Finally, in Gobaad, a <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> jaw was found, dating from 100,000 BCE. On <a href="/wiki/Ghoubbet-el-Kharab" title="Ghoubbet-el-Kharab">Devil's Island</a>, tools dating back 6,000 years have been found, which were used to open shells. In the area at the bottom of <a href="/wiki/Ghoubbet-el-Kharab" title="Ghoubbet-el-Kharab">Goubet</a> (Dankalélo, not far from Devil's Island), circular stone structures and fragments of painted <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> have also been discovered. Previous investigators have also reported a fragmentary maxilla, attributed to an older form of <a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a> and dated to ~250 Ka, from the valley of the Dagadlé Wadi.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png/330px-Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png/495px-Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png/660px-Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png 2x" data-file-width="1980" data-file-height="1848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_of_Djibouti.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Prehistoric rock art and tombs in Djibouti.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Pottery predating the mid-2nd millennium has been found at <a href="/wiki/Asa_Koma" title="Asa Koma">Asa Koma</a>, an inland lake area on the Gobaad Plain. The site's ware is characterized by punctate and incision geometric designs, which bear a similarity to the Sabir culture phase 1 ceramics from Ma'layba in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Arabia">Southern Arabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ohvah_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ohvah-19">[18]</a></sup> Long-horned humpless cattle bones have likewise been discovered at Asa Koma, suggesting that domesticated cattle were present by around 3,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Connah_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connah-20">[19]</a></sup> Rock art of what appear to be antelopes and a giraffe are also found at <a href="/wiki/Dorra" title="Dorra">Dorra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balho" title="Balho">Balho</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Amvjaa_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amvjaa-21">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Handoga" title="Handoga">Handoga</a>, dated to the fourth millennium BCE, has in turn yielded obsidian microliths and plain ceramics used by early nomadic pastoralists with domesticated cattle.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The site of Wakrita is a small <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> establishment located on a <a href="/wiki/Wadi" title="Wadi">wadi</a> in the tectonic depression of Gobaad in Djibouti in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. The 2005 excavations<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> yielded abundant ceramics that enabled us to define one Neolithic cultural facies of this <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a>, which was also identified at the nearby site of <a href="/wiki/Asa_Koma" title="Asa Koma">Asa Koma</a>. The faunal remains confirm the importance of fishing in Neolithic settlements close to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Abbe" title="Lake Abbe">Lake Abbé</a>, but also the importance of bovine husbandry and, for the first time in this area, evidence for caprine herding practices. Radiocarbon dating places this occupation at the beginning of the 2nd millennium BCE, similar in range to Asa Koma. These two sites represent the oldest evidence of herding in the region, and they provide a better understanding of the development of Neolithic societies in this region. </p>
      <p>Up to 4000 years BCE, the region benefited from a climate very different from the one it knows today and probably close to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>. The water resources were numerous with lakes in Gobaad, lakes Assal and Abbé larger and resembling real bodies of water. The humans therefore lived by gathering, fishing and hunting. The region was populated by a very rich fauna: <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">felines</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Buffalo" class="mw-redirect" title="African Buffalo">buffaloes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhino" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhino">rhinos</a>, etc., as evidenced, for example, by the bestiary of cave paintings at <a href="/wiki/Balho" title="Balho">Balho</a>. In the 3rd and 2nd millennia BCE, few nomads settled around the lakes and practiced fishing and cattle]] breeding. The burial of an 18-year-old woman, dating from this period, as well as the bones of hunted animals, bone tools and small <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">jewels</a> have been unearthed. By about 1500 BCE, the climate was already beginning to change, with sources of fresh water becoming more scarce. Engravings show dromedaries (animal of arid zones), some of which are ridden by armed warriors. The sedentary people now returned to a |nomadic life. Stone <a href="/wiki/Tumuli" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumuli">tumuli</a> of various shapes and sheltering graves dating from this period have been unearthed all over the territory. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Punt_.282.2C500_BCE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Punt_(2,500_BCE)">Punt (2,500 BCE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Punt (2,500 BCE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:156px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_punt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Queen_of_punt.jpg" decoding="async" width="154" height="246" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="154" data-file-height="246"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_punt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Queen Ati, wife of King Perahu of <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a>, as depicted on Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a>'s temple at <a href="/wiki/Deir_el-Bahri" class="mw-redirect" title="Deir el-Bahri">Deir el-Bahri</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Together with northern Ethiopia, northern Somalia], Eritrea and the Red Sea coast of Sudan, Djibouti is considered the most likely location of the territory known to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a></i> (or <i>Ta Netjeru</i>, meaning "God's Land"). The first mention of the Land of Punt dates to the 25th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> The Puntites were a nation of people who had close relations with Ancient Egypt during the reign of the 5th dynasty Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Sahure" title="Sahure">Sahure</a> and the 18th dynasty Queen <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> According to the temple murals at <a href="/wiki/Deir_el-Bahari" title="Deir el-Bahari">Deir el-Bahari</a>, the Land of Punt was ruled at that time by King Parahu and Queen Ati.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Macrobians_.28247_BCE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Macrobians_(247_BCE)">Macrobians (247 BCE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Macrobians (247 BCE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Macrobians" title="Macrobians">Macrobians</a> (Μακροβίοι) were a legendary people and kingdom positioned in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>. Later authors (so <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Indica_(Ctesias)" title="Indica (Ctesias)">Indika</a></i>) place them in <a href="/wiki/India_(Herodotus)" title="India (Herodotus)">India</a> instead. It is one of the legendary peoples postulated at the extremity of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumene" title="Ecumene">known world</a> (from the perspective of the Greeks), in this case in the extreme south, contrasting with the <a href="/wiki/Hyperboreans" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyperboreans">Hyperboreans</a> in the extreme east. Their name is due to their legendary <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a>, an average person supposedly living to the age of 120.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> They were said to be the "tallest and handsomest of all men".<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_pg_526_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler_pg_526-27">[26]</a></sup> According to Herodotus' account, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Emperor">Persian Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a> upon his <a href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">conquest of Egypt</a> (525 BC) sent ambassadors to Macrobia, bringing luxury gifts for the Macrobian king to entice his submission. The Macrobian ruler, who was elected based at least in part on stature, replied instead with a challenge for his Persian counterpart in the form of an unstrung bow: if the Persians could manage to string it, they would have the right to invade his country; but until then, they should thank the gods that the Macrobians never decided to invade their empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_pg_526_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler_pg_526-27">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitto2_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitto2-28">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Adal_Kingdom.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Adal_Kingdom.png/170px-Adal_Kingdom.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Adal_Kingdom.png/255px-Adal_Kingdom.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Adal_Kingdom.png/340px-Adal_Kingdom.png 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="1166"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Adal_Kingdom.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Approximate extension of the Kingdom of Adal
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span id="Kingdom_of_Adal_.28900.E2.80.931285.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Adal_(900–1285)">Kingdom of Adal (900–1285)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Adal (900–1285)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The <i>Kingdom of Adal</i> (also <i>Awdal</i>, <i>Adl</i>, or <i>Adel</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Somalia_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Somalia-29">[28]</a></sup> was centered around <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>, its capital.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> It was established by the local <a href="/wiki/Somalis" title="Somalis">Somali</a> tribes in the early 9th century. Zeila attracted merchants from around the world, contributing to the wealth of the city. Zeila is an ancient city and it was one of the earliest cities in the world to embrace <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">hijra</a>. <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>'s two-<a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a> <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Qiblatayn_(Somalia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Masjid al-Qiblatayn (Somalia)">Masjid al-Qiblatayn</a> dates to the 7th century, and is the oldest mosque.<sup id="cite_ref-Btgpb_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Btgpb-33">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Historical_sources_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historical_sources-34">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Somalia_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Somalia-29">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the late 9th century, <a href="/wiki/Al-Yaqubi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Yaqubi">Al-Yaqubi</a>, an Armenian Muslim scholar and traveler, wrote that the Kingdom of Adal was a small wealthy kingdom and that Zeila served as the headquarters for the kingdom, which dated back to the beginning of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyamer1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyamer1-36">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewispohoa_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispohoa-37">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Ifat_Sultanate_.281285.E2.80.931415.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ifat_Sultanate_(1285–1415)">Ifat Sultanate (1285–1415)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ifat Sultanate (1285–1415)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifat Sultanate">Ifat Sultanate</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png/220px-The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png/330px-The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png/440px-The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png 2x" data-file-width="3527" data-file-height="3503"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:The_Sultanate_of_Ifat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifat Sultanate">Ifat Sultanate</a>'s realm in the 14th century.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Through close contacts with the adjacent Arabian Peninsula for more than 1,000 years, the Somali and Afar ethnic groups in the region became among the first populations on the continent to embrace Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ifat_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifat Sultanate">Ifat Sultanate</a> was a Muslim medieval kingdom in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. Founded in 1285 by the <a href="/wiki/Walashma_dynasty" title="Walashma dynasty">Walashma dynasty</a>, it was centered in Zeila.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Ifat established bases in Djibouti and northern Somalia, and from there expanded southward to the <a href="/wiki/Ahmar_Mountains" title="Ahmar Mountains">Ahmar Mountains</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> Umar Walashma (or his son Ali, according to another source) is recorded as having conquered the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Showa" title="Sultanate of Showa">Sultanate of Shewa</a> in 1285. Taddesse Tamrat explains Sultan Umar's military expedition as an effort to consolidate the Muslim territories in the Horn, in much the same way as Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yekuno_Amlak" title="Yekuno Amlak">Yekuno Amlak</a> was attempting to unite the Christian territories in the highlands during the same period. These two states inevitably came into conflict over Shewa and territories further south. A lengthy war ensued, but the Muslim sultanates of the time were not strongly unified. Ifat was finally defeated by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Amda_Seyon_I" title="Amda Seyon I">Amda Seyon I</a> of Ethiopia in 1332, and withdrew from Shewa. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Adal_Sultanate_.281415.E2.80.931577.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Adal_Sultanate_(1415–1577)">Adal Sultanate (1415–1577)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Adal Sultanate (1415–1577)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG/220px-YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG/330px-YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG/440px-YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2379" data-file-height="1524"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:YagbeaSionBattlingAdaSultan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Sultan of Adal</a> (right) and his troops battling King <a href="/wiki/Yagbe%27u_Seyon_of_Ethiopia" class="mw-redirect" title="Yagbe'u Seyon of Ethiopia">Yagbea-Sion</a> and his men.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to the 16th-century explorer <a href="/wiki/Leo_Africanus" title="Leo Africanus">Leo Africanus</a>, the Adal Sultanate's realm encompassed the geographical area between the <a href="/wiki/Bab_el_Mandeb" class="mw-redirect" title="Bab el Mandeb">Bab el Mandeb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Guardafui" title="Cape Guardafui">Cape Guardafui</a>. It was therefore flanked to the south by the <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Ajuran Empire">Ajuran Empire</a> (Kingdom of Ajuuran) and to the west by the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Abyssinian Empire">Abyssinian Empire</a> (Abassin Empire).<sup id="cite_ref-Leo_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leo-41">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michigan_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michigan-42">[41]</a></sup> Adal is mentioned by name in the 14th century in the context of the battles between the Muslims of the Somali and Afar seaboard and the Abyssinian King <a href="/wiki/Amda_Seyon_I" title="Amda Seyon I">Amda Seyon I</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> troops.<sup id="cite_ref-Houtsma_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houtsma-43">[42]</a></sup> Adal originally had its capital in the port city of Zeila, situated in the northwestern Awdal region. The polity at the time was an <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emirate</a> in the larger <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Ifat" title="Sultanate of Ifat">Ifat Sultanate</a> ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Walashma_dynasty" title="Walashma dynasty">Walashma dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewispd_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispd-44">[43]</a></sup> According to I.M. Lewis, the polity was governed by local dynasties consisting of Afarized Arabs or Arabized Somalis, who also ruled over the similarly-established <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mogadishu" title="Sultanate of Mogadishu">Sultanate of Mogadishu</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Benadir" class="mw-redirect" title="Benadir">Benadir</a> region to the south. Adal's history from this founding period forth would be characterized by a succession of battles with neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewispohoa_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewispohoa-37">[36]</a></sup> At its height, the Adal kingdom controlled large parts of modern-day Djibouti, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and Ethiopia. Between <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Djibouti City">Djibouti City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loyada" title="Loyada">Loyada</a> are a number of anthropomorphic and phallic <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stelae</a>. The structures are associated with graves of rectangular shape flanked by vertical slabs, as also found in <a href="/wiki/Tiya" title="Tiya">Tiya</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. The Djibouti-Loyada stelae are of uncertain age, and some of them are adorned with a T-shaped symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-Fattovich_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fattovich-45">[44]</a></sup> Additionally, archaeological excavations at Tiya have yielded tombs.<sup id="cite_ref-Utps_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utps-46">[45]</a></sup> As of 1997, 118 stelae were reported in the area. Along with the stelae in the <a href="/wiki/Hadiya_Zone" title="Hadiya Zone">Hadiya Zone</a>, the structures are identified by local residents as <i>Yegragn Dingay</i> or "Gran's stone", in reference to Imam <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Ibrahim_al-Ghazi" title="Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi">Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi</a> (Ahmad "Gurey" or "Gran"), ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fukui_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fukui-47">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Ottoman_Eyalet_.281577.E2.80.931867.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Eyalet_(1577–1867)">Ottoman Eyalet (1577–1867)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ottoman Eyalet (1577–1867)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Egypt Eyalet</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:OttomanEmpire1566.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/OttomanEmpire1566.png/220px-OttomanEmpire1566.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/OttomanEmpire1566.png/330px-OttomanEmpire1566.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/OttomanEmpire1566.png/440px-OttomanEmpire1566.png 2x" data-file-width="3596" data-file-height="2581"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:OttomanEmpire1566.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Ottoman Eyalet</a> in 1566.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Although nominally part of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> since 1577, between 1821 and 1841, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a>, Pasha of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, came to control <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harar" title="Harar">Harar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tadjoura" title="Gulf of Tadjoura">Gulf of Tadjoura</a> with <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a> included. The Governor Abou Baker ordered the Egyptian garrison at <a href="/wiki/Sagallo" title="Sagallo">Sagallo</a> to retire to <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>. The cruiser Seignelay reached Sagallo shortly after the Egyptians had departed. French troops occupied the fort despite protests from the British Agent in <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a>, Major Frederick Mercer Hunter, who dispatched troops to safeguard British and Egyptian interests in <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a> and prevent further extension of French influence in that direction.<sup id="cite_ref-awdalpress.com_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awdalpress.com-48">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 14 April 1884 the Commander of the patrol sloop L'Inferent reported on the Egyptian occupation in the Gulf of Tadjoura. The Commander of the patrol sloop Le Vaudreuil reported that the Egyptians were occupying the interior between <a href="/wiki/Obock" title="Obock">Obock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tadjoura" title="Tadjoura">Tadjoura</a>. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yohannes_IV" title="Yohannes IV">Yohannes IV</a> of Ethiopia signed an accord with Great Britain to cease fighting the Egyptians and to allow the evacuation of Egyptian forces from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the Somalia littoral. The Egyptian garrison was withdrawn from <a href="/wiki/Tadjoura" title="Tadjoura">Tadjoura</a>. Léonce Lagarde deployed a patrol sloop to <a href="/wiki/Tadjoura" title="Tadjoura">Tadjoura</a> the following night. </p> 
      <h3><span id="French_Somaliland_.281883.E2.80.931977.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Somaliland_(1883–1977)">French Somaliland (1883–1977)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: French Somaliland (1883–1977)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_(...)Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg/220px-Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg/330px-Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg/440px-Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_%28...%29Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1399" data-file-height="1054"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Carte_de_la_c%C3%B4te_fran%C3%A7aise_(...)Meunier_Alexandre_btv1b53062311r_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of the French Somali Coast and neighboring regions in 1870
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="515"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:A-demonstration-in-Djibouti-Africa-1967-352022097779_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Referendum demonstration in Djibouti in 1967
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The boundaries of the present-day Djibouti state were established as the first French establishment in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>. The March 11, 1862, agreement the <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">sultan</a>, Raieta Dini Ahmet, signed in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> was a treaty where the Afars sold lands surrounding in <a href="/wiki/Obock" title="Obock">Obock</a>. The French were interested in having a <a href="/wiki/Coaling_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Coaling station">coaling station</a> for <a href="/wiki/Steamship" title="Steamship">steamships</a>, which would become especially important upon the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in 1869. (Up to that time French ships had to buy coal at the British port of <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> across the gulf, an unwise dependency in case of war.) Later on, that treaty was used by the captain of the Fleuriot de Langle to colonize the south of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tadjoura" title="Gulf of Tadjoura">Gulf of Tadjoura</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> On March 26, 1885 the French signed another treaty with the Issas where the latter would become a protectorate under the French, no monetary exchange occurred and <a href="/wiki/Issa_clan" class="mw-redirect" title="Issa clan">Issa clan</a> did not sign away any of their rights to the land, the agreement was to protect their land from outsiders with the help of the French.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> It was established between 1883 and 1887, after the ruling <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somalis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a> sultans each signed a treaty with the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisholm_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisholm-53">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uwechue_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uwechue-6">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-8">[7]</a></sup> An attempt by Nikolay Ivanovitch Achinov, a <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> adventurer, to establish a settlement at <a href="/wiki/Sagallo" title="Sagallo">Sagallo</a> in 1889 was promptly thwarted by French forces after just one month. In 1894, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9once_Lagarde" title="Léonce Lagarde">Léonce Lagarde</a> established a permanent French administration in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Djibouti" class="mw-redirect" title="City of Djibouti">city of Djibouti</a> and named the region <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a>. As is shown in "Morin" (2005), this name has been proposed by Mohamed Haji Dide of the Mahad 'Ase branch of the <a href="/wiki/Gadabuursi" title="Gadabuursi">Gadabuursi</a>. It lasted from 1896 until 1967, when it was renamed the <i>Territoire Français des Afars et des Issas</i> (TFAI) ("<a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a>"), after France, the colonial power, has empowered the Issas clan at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/Gadabuursi" title="Gadabuursi">Gadabuursi</a>. The construction of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Ethiopian_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Ethiopian Railway">Imperial Ethiopian Railway</a> west into <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> turned the <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">port of Djibouti</a> into a boomtown of 15,000<sup id="cite_ref-EBD_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBD-54">[53]</a></sup> at a time when <a href="/wiki/Harar" title="Harar">Harar</a> was the only city in Ethiopia to exceed that.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Although the population fell after the completion of the railwayline to <a href="/wiki/Dire_Dawa" title="Dire Dawa">Dire Dawa</a> and the original company failed and required a government bail-out, the rail link allowed the territory to quickly supersede the caravan-based trade carried on at <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> (then in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a> area of <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">Somaliland</a>) and become the premier port for <a href="/wiki/Harari_coffee" class="mw-redirect" title="Harari coffee">coffee</a> and other goods leaving southern Ethiopia and the <a href="/wiki/Ogaden" title="Ogaden">Ogaden</a> through <a href="/wiki/Harar" title="Harar">Harar</a>. </p>
      <p>After the Italian <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">invasion and occupation of Ethiopia</a> in the mid-1930s, constant border skirmishes occurred between French forces in French Somaliland and Italian forces in <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">Italian East Africa</a>. In June 1940, during the early stages of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of France">France fell</a> and the colony was then ruled by the pro-<a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis</a> <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy (French) government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImbert-Vier2008226–29_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImbert-Vier2008226–29-57">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>British and Commonwealth forces fought the neighboring Italians during the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">East African Campaign</a>. In 1941, the Italians were defeated and the Vichy forces in French Somaliland were isolated. The Vichy French administration continued to hold out in the colony for over a year after the Italian collapse. In response, the British blockaded the port of <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Djibouti City">Djibouti City</a> but it could not prevent local French from providing information on the passing ship convoys. In 1942, about 4,000 <a href="/wiki/British_troops" class="mw-redirect" title="British troops">British troops</a> occupied the city. A local battalion from French Somaliland participated in the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_of_Paris" title="Liberation of Paris">Liberation of Paris</a> in 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbsworth1953564_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbsworth1953564-58">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1958, on the eve of neighboring Somalia's independence in 1960, a <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland_overseas_territory_referendum,_1958" class="mw-redirect" title="French Somaliland overseas territory referendum, 1958">referendum</a> was held in Djibouti to decide whether to remain with France or to be an independent country. The referendum turned out in favour of a continued association with France, partly due to a combined yes vote by the sizable Afar ethnic group and resident French.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-59">[58]</a></sup> There were also allegations of widespread <a href="/wiki/Vote_rigging" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote rigging">vote rigging</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> The majority of those who had voted no were Somalis who were strongly in favour of joining a united Somalia as had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Harbi" title="Mahmoud Harbi">Mahmoud Harbi</a>, Vice President of the Government Council. Harbi was killed in a plane crash two years later under suspicious circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-59">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg/220px-An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg/330px-An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg/440px-An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="540"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:An_aerial_view_of_Djibouti_City.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_(city)" title="Djibouti (city)">Djibouti City</a>, the capital of Djibouti.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1966, France rejected the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' recommendation that it should grant French Somaliland independence. In August of the same year, an official visit to the territory by then French President, General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>, was also met with demonstrations and rioting.<sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nwvol_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nwvol-61">[60]</a></sup> In response to the protests, de Gaulle ordered another referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-Nwvol_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nwvol-61">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1967, a <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland_independence_referendum,_1967" class="mw-redirect" title="French Somaliland independence referendum, 1967">second plebiscite</a> was held to determine the fate of the territory. Initial results supported a continued but looser relationship with France. Voting was also divided along ethnic lines, with the resident Somalis generally voting for independence, with the goal of eventual union with Somalia, and the Afars largely opting to remain associated with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Apcoatf_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apcoatf-8">[7]</a></sup> The referendum was again marred by reports of vote rigging on the part of the French authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Aufsnas_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aufsnas-62">[61]</a></sup> Shortly after the plebiscite was held, the former <i>Côte française des Somalis</i> (French Somaliland) was renamed to <i>Territoire français des Afars et des Issas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tioipeami_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tioipeami-63">[62]</a></sup> Announcement of the plebiscite results sparked civil unrest, including several deaths. France also increased its military force along the frontier.<sup id="cite_ref-Tioipeami_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tioipeami-63">[62]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis_Symbol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis_Symbol.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="226" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="226"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis_Symbol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis" title="Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis">Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis (FLCS)</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the 1960s, the struggle for independence was led by the <a href="/wiki/Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis" title="Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis">Front for the Liberation of the Somali Coast</a> (FLCS), who waged an armed struggle for independence with much of its violence aimed at French personnel. FLCS used to initiate few mounting cross-border operations into <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> from <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somalia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Derg" title="Derg">Ethiopia</a> to attacks on French targets. On March 24, 1975 the Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis kidnapped the French Ambassador to Somalia, Jean Guery, to be exchanged against two activists of FLCS members who were both serving life terms in mainland <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. He was exchanged for the two FLCS members in <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Yemen" title="South Yemen">South Yemen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> The FLCS was recognized as a national liberation movement by the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_African_Unity" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of African Unity">Organization of African Unity</a> (OAU), which participated in its financing. The FLCS evolved its demands between the request of integration in a possible "<a href="/wiki/Greater_Somalia" title="Greater Somalia">Greater Somalia</a>" influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somali government</a> or the simple independence of the territory. In 1975 the African People's League for the Independence (LPAI) and FLCS met in <a href="/wiki/Kampala" title="Kampala">Kampala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> with several meeting later they finally opted for independence path, causing tensions with <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somalia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1976, members of the <a href="/wiki/Front_de_Lib%C3%A9ration_de_la_C%C3%B4te_des_Somalis" title="Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis">Front de Libération de la Côte des Somalis</a> which sought Djibouti's independence from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, also clashed with the Gendarmerie Nationale Intervention Group over a bus hijacking en route to <a href="/wiki/Loyada" title="Loyada">Loyada</a>. This event, by showing the difficulties of maintaining the French colonial presence in Djibouti, was an important step in the <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Territory" title="Territory">territory</a>. The likelihood of a third <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> appearing successful for the French had grown even dimmer. The prohibitive cost of maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colony</a>, France's last outpost on the <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a>, was another factor that compelled observers to doubt that the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> would attempt to hold on to the territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Nwvol_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nwvol-61">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Djibouti_Republic">Djibouti Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Djibouti Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg/220px-Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg/330px-Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg/440px-Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="732"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed_proclaiming_the_Djibouti_Declaration_of_Independence_on_27_June_1977.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Dini_Ahmed" title="Ahmed Dini Ahmed">Ahmed Dini Ahmed</a> proclaiming the Djibouti Declaration of Independence on 27 June 1977.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/1977_Afars_and_Issas_independence_referendum" title="1977 Afars and Issas independence referendum">third independence referendum</a> was held in the <a href="/wiki/French_Territory_of_the_Afars_and_the_Issas" title="French Territory of the Afars and the Issas">French Territory of the Afars and the Issas</a> on 8 May 1977. The previous referendums were held in <a href="/wiki/1958_French_Somaliland_overseas_territory_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="1958 French Somaliland overseas territory referendum">1958</a> and <a href="/wiki/1967_French_Somaliland_independence_referendum" title="1967 French Somaliland independence referendum">1967</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kseoah_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kseoah-67">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aufsnas_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aufsnas-62">[61]</a></sup> which rejected <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a>. This referendum backed independence from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> A landslide 98.8% of the electorate supported disengagement from France, officially marking <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Djibouti)" title="Independence Day (Djibouti)">Djibouti's independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nwvol_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nwvol-61">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon" title="Hassan Gouled Aptidon">Hassan Gouled Aptidon</a>, a Djiboutian politician who had campaigned for a yes vote in the referendum of 1958, became the nation's first president (1977–1999).<sup id="cite_ref-Barrington2006_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrington2006-59">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During its first year, Djibouti joined the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_African_Unity" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of African Unity">Organization of African Unity</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> and United Nations. In 1986, the nascent republic was also among the founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Authority_on_Development" title="Intergovernmental Authority on Development">Intergovernmental Authority on Development</a> regional development organization. </p>
      <p>In the early 1990s, tensions over government representation led to <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_Civil_War" title="Djiboutian Civil War">armed conflict</a> between Djibouti's ruling <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Rally_for_Progress" title="People's Rally for Progress">People's Rally for Progress</a> (PRP) party and the <a href="/wiki/Front_for_the_Restoration_of_Unity_and_Democracy" title="Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy">Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy</a> (FRUD) opposition group. The impasse ended in a power-sharing agreement in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Djibouti" title="Politics of Djibouti">Politics of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Djibouti" title="Elections in Djibouti">Elections in Djibouti</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Djiboutian_politicians" title="List of Djiboutian politicians">List of Djiboutian politicians</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Djibouti is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Executive_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive power">executive power</a> resting in the presidency, which is by turn dominant over the cabinet, and <a href="/wiki/Legislative_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative power">legislative power</a> in both the government and the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Djibouti)" title="National Assembly (Djibouti)">National Assembly</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/170px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/255px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg/340px-Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="1332"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Omar_Guelleh_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>President of Djibouti, <a href="/wiki/Isma%C3%AFl_Omar_Guelleh" title="Ismaïl Omar Guelleh">Ismaïl Omar Guelleh</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Djibouti" class="mw-redirect" title="President of Djibouti">President</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isma%C3%AFl_Omar_Guelleh" title="Ismaïl Omar Guelleh">Ismaïl Omar Guelleh</a>, is the prominent figure in Djiboutian politics; the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a>. The President exercises their executive power assisted by their appointee, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Djibouti" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister of Djibouti">Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed" title="Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed">Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed</a>. The Council of Ministers (cabinet) is responsible to and presided over by the President. </p>
      <p>The judicial system consists of courts of first instance, a High Court of Appeal, and a Supreme Court. The <a href="/wiki/Legal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal system">legal system</a> is a blend of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">French civil law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Customary_law" title="Customary law">customary law</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Xeer" title="Xeer">Xeer</a></i>) of the Somali and Afar peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-English_constitution_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_constitution-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-constitution_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-70">[69]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The National Assembly (formerly the <i>Chamber of Deputies</i>) is the country's legislature,<sup id="cite_ref-English_constitution_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_constitution-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-constitution_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-70">[69]</a></sup> consisting of 65 members elected every five years.<sup id="cite_ref-freedomhouse_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomhouse-71">[70]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a>, the Constitution provides for the creation of a Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-English_constitution_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_constitution-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-constitution_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-70">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:180px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed.jpg" decoding="async" width="178" height="269" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="178" data-file-height="269"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed" title="Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed">Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed</a>, Prime Minister of Djibouti.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_parliamentary_election,_2018" class="mw-redirect" title="Djiboutian parliamentary election, 2018">last election</a> was held on 23 February 2018. Djibouti has a <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system">dominant-party system</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Rally_for_Progress" title="People's Rally for Progress">People's Rally for Progress</a> (RPP) controlling the legislature and the executive since its foundation in 1979 (the party rules as a part of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Presidential_Majority" class="mw-redirect" title="Union for a Presidential Majority">Union for a Presidential Majority</a>, which holds a <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">supermajority</a> of seats). Opposition parties are allowed (limited) freedom, but the main opposition party, the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_National_Salvation" title="Union for National Salvation">Union for National Salvation</a>, boycotted the 2005 and 2008 elections, citing government control of the media and repression of the opposition candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-freedomhouse_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomhouse-71">[70]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government is dominated by the Somali <a href="/wiki/Issa_(clan)" title="Issa (clan)">Issa</a> <a href="/wiki/Dir_(clan)" title="Dir (clan)">Dir</a> clan, who enjoy the support of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_clan" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali clan">Somali clans</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Gadabuursi" title="Gadabuursi">Gadabuursi</a> <a href="/wiki/Dir_(clan)" title="Dir (clan)">Dir</a> clan. The country emerged from a decade-long <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_Civil_War" title="Djiboutian Civil War">civil war</a> at the end of the 1990s, with the government and the <a href="/wiki/Front_for_the_Restoration_of_Unity_and_Democracy" title="Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy">Front for the Restoration of Unity and Democracy</a> (FRUD) signing a peace treaty in 2000. Two FRUD members subsequently joined the cabinet,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> and beginning with <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_1999" class="mw-redirect" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 1999">the presidential elections of 1999</a>, the FRUD has campaigned in support of the RPP. </p>
      <p>Djibouti's president, Guelleh, succeeded <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon" title="Hassan Gouled Aptidon">Hassan Gouled Aptidon</a> in office in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-IRIN_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-72">[71]</a></sup> Guelleh was sworn in for his second six-year term after a <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 2005">one-man election</a> on 8 April 2005. He took 100% of the votes in a 78.9% turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-IRIN_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-72">[71]</a></sup> In early 2011, the Djiboutian citizenry took part in a <a href="/wiki/2011_Djiboutian_protests" title="2011 Djiboutian protests">series of protests</a> against the long-serving government, which were associated with the larger <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> demonstrations. Guelleh was <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_presidential_election,_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="Djiboutian presidential election, 2011">re-elected</a> to a third term later that year, with 80.63% of the vote in a 75% turnout.<sup id="cite_ref-Meodvpe_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meodvpe-73">[72]</a></sup> Although opposition groups boycotted the ballot over changes to the constitution permitting Guelleh to run again for office,<sup id="cite_ref-Meodvpe_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meodvpe-73">[72]</a></sup> international observers from the African Union generally described the election as free and fair.<sup id="cite_ref-Spabcdpohr_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spabcdpohr-74">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 31 March 2013, Guelleh replaced long-serving Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Dilleita_Mohamed_Dilleita" class="mw-redirect" title="Dilleita Mohamed Dilleita">Dilleita Mohamed Dilleita</a> with former president of the Union for a Presidential Majority (UMP) <a href="/wiki/Abdoulkader_Kamil_Mohamed" title="Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed">Abdoulkader Kamil Mohamed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makmgcdlenpmd_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makmgcdlenpmd-76">[75]</a></sup> In December 2014, the ruling Union for the Presidential Majority also signed a framework agreement with the Union of National Salvation coalition, which paves the way for opposition legislators to enter parliament and for reformation of the national electoral agency.<sup id="cite_ref-Gdgrdtboip_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gdgrdtboip-77">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Djibouti" title="Foreign relations of Djibouti">Foreign relations of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG/220px-DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG/330px-DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG/440px-DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3228" data-file-height="1849"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:DjiboutiNationalAssembly.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Djibouti National Assembly in Djibouti City.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Foreign relations of Djibouti are managed by the Djiboutian Ministry of Foreign Affairs and International Cooperation. Djibouti maintains close ties with the governments of <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. It is likewise an active participant in <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> affairs. Since the 2000s, Djiboutian authorities have also strengthened relations with <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Djibouti" title="Human rights in Djibouti">Human rights in Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In its 2011 Freedom in the World report, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> ranked Djibouti as "Not Free", a downgrading from its former status as "Partly Free". </p>
