      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Djibouti" title="Health in Djibouti">Health in Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Isssdjib1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Isssdjib1.jpg/170px-Isssdjib1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Isssdjib1.jpg/255px-Isssdjib1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Isssdjib1.jpg/340px-Isssdjib1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Isssdjib1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Entrance to the ISSS Faculty of Medicine in Djibouti City.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> at birth is around 64.7 for both males and females. Fertility is at 2.35 children per woman.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> In Djibouti there are about 18 doctors per 100,000 persons.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 2010 maternal mortality rate per 100,000 births for Djibouti is 300. This is compared with 461.6 in 2008 and 606.5 in 1990. The under 5 mortality rate, per 1,000 births is 95 and the neonatal mortality as a percentage of under 5's mortality are 37. In Djibouti the number of midwives per 1,000 live births is 6 and the lifetime risk of death for pregnant women 1 in 93.<sup id="cite_ref-SOWMY_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOWMY-135">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>About 93.1% of Djibouti's women and girls have undergone <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> (female circumcision),<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> a pre-marital custom mainly endemic to Northeast Africa and parts of the Near East.<sup id="cite_ref-Rohayes_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rohayes-137">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bodman_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodman-138">[137]</a></sup> Although legally proscribed in 1994, the procedure is still widely practiced, as it is deeply ingrained in the local culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Djiwfm_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Djiwfm-139">[138]</a></sup> Encouraged and performed by women in the community, circumcision is primarily intended to deter promiscuity and to offer protection from assault.<sup id="cite_ref-Djiwfm_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Djiwfm-139">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frayser_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frayser-140">[139]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>About 94% of Djibouti's male population have also reportedly undergone <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">male circumcision</a>, a figure in line with adherence to Islam, which requires this.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahuja_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahuja-141">[140]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Djibouti" title="Education in Djibouti">Education in Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Education is a priority for the government of Djibouti. As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it allocates 20.5% of its annual budget to scholastic instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-142">[141]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Djibwomglopul.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Djibwomglopul.jpeg/220px-Djibwomglopul.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Djibwomglopul.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="265"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Djibwomglopul.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Djiboutian women participating in the Global Pulse educational initiative (2010).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Djiboutian educational system was initially formulated to cater to a limited pupil base. As such, the schooling framework was largely elitist and drew considerably from the French colonial paradigm, which was ill-suited to local circumstances and needs.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-142">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the late 1990s, the Djiboutian authorities revised the national educational strategy and launched a broad-based consultative process involving administrative officials, teachers, parents, national assembly members and NGOs. The initiative identified areas in need of attention and produced concrete recommendations on how to go about improving them. The government subsequently prepared a comprehensive reform plan aimed at modernizing the educational sector over the 2000–10 period. In August 2000, it passed an official Education Planning Act and drafted a medium-term development plan for the next five years. The fundamental academic system was significantly restructured and made compulsory; it now consists of five years of primary school and four years of middle school. Secondary schools also require a Certificate of Fundamental Education for admission. In addition, the new law introduced secondary-level vocational instruction and established university facilities in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-142">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As a result of the Education Planning Act and the medium-term action strategy, substantial progress has been registered throughout the educational sector.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_142-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-142">[141]</a></sup> In particular, school enrollment, attendance, and retention rates have all steadily increased, with some regional variation. From 2004 to 2005 to 2007–08, net enrollments of girls in primary school rose by 18.6%; for boys, it increased 8.0%. Net enrollments in middle school over the same period rose by 72.4% for girls and 52.2% for boys. At the secondary level, the rate of increase in net enrollments was 49.8% for girls and 56.1% for boys.<sup id="cite_ref-Datee_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Datee-143">[142]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Djiboutian government has especially focused on developing and improving institutional infrastructure and teaching materials, including constructing new classrooms and supplying textbooks. At the post-secondary level, emphasis has also been placed on producing qualified instructors and encouraging out-of-school youngsters to pursue vocational training.<sup id="cite_ref-infodev.org_142-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infodev.org-142">[141]</a></sup> As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the literacy rate in Djibouti was estimated at 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-Pcaotw_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pcaotw-144">[143]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Institutions of higher learning in the country include the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Djibouti" title="University of Djibouti">University of Djibouti</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Djibouti" title="Culture of Djibouti">Culture of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a,_Tadjoura,_Djibouti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/150px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/225px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg/300px-Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a%2C_Tadjoura%2C_Djibouti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wood_jar_from_Oue%60a,_Tadjoura,_Djibouti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Traditional wood-carved jar from <a href="/wiki/Oued_Ouea" title="Oued Ouea">Oue'a</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tadjourah_Region" title="Tadjourah Region">Tadjourah</a> region.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Djiboutian attire reflects the region's hot and arid climate. When not dressed in Western clothing such as jeans and T-shirts, men typically wear the <i lang="aa-Latn" title="Afar-language romanization">macawiis</i>, which is a traditional <a href="/wiki/Sarong" title="Sarong">sarong</a>-like garment worn around the waist. Many nomadic people wear a loosely wrapped white cotton robe called a <i>tobe</i> that goes down to about the knee, with the end thrown over the shoulder (much like a Roman <a href="/wiki/Toga" title="Toga">toga</a>). </p>
      <p>Women typically wear the <i>dirac</i>, which is a long, light, diaphanous <a href="/wiki/Voile" title="Voile">voile</a> dress made of cotton or polyester that is worn over a full-length <a href="/wiki/Slip_(clothing)" title="Slip (clothing)">half-slip</a> and a bra. Married women tend to sport head-scarves referred to as <i>shash</i> and often cover their upper body with a <a href="/wiki/Shawl" title="Shawl">shawl</a> known as <i>garbasaar</i>. Unmarried or young women, however, do not always cover their heads. Traditional Arabian garb such as the male <a href="/wiki/Jellabiya" title="Jellabiya">jellabiya</a> (<i>jellabiyaad</i> in Somali) and the female <a href="/wiki/Jilb%C4%81b" title="Jilbāb">jilbāb</a> is also commonly worn. For some occasions such as festivals, women may adorn themselves with specialized jewelry and head-dresses similar to those worn by the <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Berber</a> tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A lot of Djibouti's original art is passed on and preserved orally, mainly through song. Many examples of Islamic, Ottoman, and French influences can also be noted in the local buildings, which contain plasterwork, carefully constructed <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motifs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Djibouti" title="Music of Djibouti">Music of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg/130px-Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg/195px-Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg/260px-Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg 2x" data-file-width="383" data-file-height="640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Oud_-_MIM_PHX.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">oud</a> is a common instrument in traditional Djibouti music.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Somalis have a rich musical heritage centered on traditional Somali <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>. Most Somali songs are <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic</a>. That is, they only use five <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitches</a> per <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> in contrast to a <a href="/wiki/Heptatonic_scale" title="Heptatonic scale">heptatonic</a> (seven note) scale such as the <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major scale</a>. At first listen, Somali music might be mistaken for the sounds of nearby regions such as Ethiopia, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, but it is ultimately recognizable by its own unique tunes and styles. Somali songs are usually the product of collaboration between lyricists (<i lang="aa-Latn" title="Afar-language romanization">midho</i>), songwriters (<i lang="aa-Latn" title="Afar-language romanization">laxan</i>) and singers (<i lang="aa-Latn" title="Afar-language romanization">codka</i> or "voice"). <a href="/wiki/Balwo" title="Balwo">Balwo</a> is a Somali musical style centered on love themes that is popular in Djibouti.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Traditional Afar music resembles the folk music of other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ethiopia" title="Music of Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>; it also contains elements of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a>. The history of Djibouti is recorded in the poetry and songs of its nomadic people, and goes back thousands of years to a time when the peoples of Djibouti traded hides and skins for the perfumes and spices of ancient <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and China. Afar oral literature is also quite musical. It comes in many varieties, including songs for weddings, war, praise and boasting.<sup id="cite_ref-expedition_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expedition-147">[146]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Djibouti" title="Literature of Djibouti">Literature of Djibouti</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Djibouti has a long tradition of poetry. Several well-developed Somali forms of verse include the <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">gabay</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">jiifto</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">geeraar</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">wiglo</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">'buraanbur</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">beercade</i>, <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">afarey</i> and <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">guuraw</i>. The <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">gabay</i> (epic poem) has the most complex length and meter, often exceeding 100 lines. It is considered the mark of poetic attainment when a young poet is able to compose such verse, and is regarded as the height of poetry. Groups of memorizers and reciters (<i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">hafidayaal</i>) traditionally propagated the well-developed art form. Poems revolve around several main themes, including <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">baroorodiiq</i> (elegy), <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">amaan</i> (praise), <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">jacayl</i> (romance), <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">guhaadin</i> (diatribe), <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">digasho</i> (gloating) and <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">guubaabo</i> (guidance). The baroorodiiq is composed to commemorate the death of a prominent poet or figure.<sup id="cite_ref-Abdullahi75_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdullahi75-148">[147]</a></sup> The Afar are familiar with the <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">ginnili</i>, a kind of warrior-poet and diviner, and have a rich oral tradition of folk stories. They also have an extensive repertoire of battle songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-149">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Additionally, Djibouti has a long tradition of Islamic literature. Among the most prominent historical works is the medieval <i>Futuh Al-Habash</i> by Shihāb al-Dīn, which chronicles the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a> army's <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian-Adal_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian-Adal War">conquest of Abyssinia</a> during the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Shihab_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shihab-150">[149]</a></sup> In recent years, a number of politicians and intellectuals have also penned memoirs or reflections on the country. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg/220px-El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg/330px-El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg/440px-El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="361"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/El_Hadj_Hassan_Gouled_Aptidon_Stadium" title="El Hadj Hassan Gouled Aptidon Stadium">El Hadj Hassan Gouled Aptidon Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Djibouti City">Djibouti City</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Football is the most popular sport amongst Djiboutians. The country became a member of <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> in 1994, but has only taken part in the qualifying rounds for the <a href="/wiki/African_Cup_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="African Cup of Nations">African Cup of Nations</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> in the mid-2000s. In November 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Djibouti_national_football_team" title="Djibouti national football team">Djibouti national football team</a> beat <a href="/wiki/Somalia_national_football_team" title="Somalia national football team">Somalia's national squad</a> 1–0 in the qualification rounds for the <a href="/wiki/2010_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2010 FIFA World Cup">2010 FIFA World Cup</a>, marking its first ever World Cup-related win.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Recently, the <a href="/wiki/World_Archery_Federation" title="World Archery Federation">World Archery Federation</a> has helped to implement the Djibouti Archery Federation, and an international archery training center is being created in <a href="/wiki/Arta,_Djibouti" title="Arta, Djibouti">Arta</a> to support archery development in East Africa and Red Sea area.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_cuisine" title="Djiboutian cuisine">Djiboutian cuisine</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sambusadish.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/200px-Sambusadish.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/300px-Sambusadish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Sambusadish.jpg/400px-Sambusadish.jpg 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="374"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sambusadish.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A plate of <i><a href="/wiki/Samosa" title="Samosa">sambusas</a></i> a popular traditional snack.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Djiboutian_cuisine" title="Djiboutian cuisine">Djiboutian cuisine</a> is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Somali_cuisine" title="Somali cuisine">Somali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afar_people" title="Afar people">Afar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_cuisine" title="Yemeni cuisine">Yemeni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_cuisine" title="French cuisine">French cuisine</a>, with some additional <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asian cuisine">South Asian</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a>) culinary influences. Local dishes are commonly prepared using a lot of Middle Eastern spices, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Saffron" title="Saffron">saffron</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>. Grilled Yemeni fish, opened in half and often cooked in tandoori style ovens, are a local delicacy. Spicy dishes come in many variations, from the traditional <i>Fah-fah</i> or "<i>Soupe Djiboutienne</i>" (spicy boiled beef soup), to the <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">yetakelt wet</i> (spicy mixed vegetable stew). <i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">Xalwo</i> (pronounced "halwo") or <a href="/wiki/Halva" title="Halva">halva</a> is a popular confection eaten during festive occasions, such as <a href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" class="mw-redirect" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid</a> celebrations or wedding receptions. Halva is made from sugar, <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardamom" title="Cardamom">cardamom</a> powder, <a href="/wiki/Nutmeg" title="Nutmeg">nutmeg</a> powder and <a href="/wiki/Ghee" title="Ghee">ghee</a>. Peanuts are sometimes added to enhance texture and flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> After meals, homes are traditionally perfumed using <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a> (<i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">cuunsi</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a> (<i lang="so-Latn" title="Somali-language romanization">lubaan</i>), which is prepared inside an incense burner referred to as a <i><a href="/wiki/Dabqaad" title="Dabqaad">dabqaad</a></i>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Djibouti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

      </ul>
